منهجي متكامل؛ فالمادة النظرية لم تكن متوفرة، وقد اقتضى مني استخلاصها سياحات طويلة في مختلف كتب علم أصول الفقه، وهي موسوعات كبيرة ولغتها صعبة وتحتاج إلى رصيد طويل من الخبرة وطول الممارسة والمادة التطبيقية متفرقة مشتتة، وقد تطلب مني جمعها كذلك جهدًا طويلا ومعاناة كبيرة.
Jadal among the usuliyyin, and I have treated these etiquettes through four investigations: the first investigation dealt with etiquettes related to the personality of the debater; the second: etiquettes related to the relationship between the two debaters; the third: etiquettes related to the subject of debate; and the fourth: etiquettes related to the style of debate. As for the second section: Applications of jadal among the usuliyyin, I concluded by induction that these applications all revolve around evidence, and they are confined to four axes of evidence: the Book, the Sunna, qiyas, and the rest of the legal evidences. Therefore, in the first chapter I studied jadal in inference from the Book, where I confined the objections raised against inference from Quranic verses and the answers to them into seven investigations: the first investigation: objection from the angle that the arguer does not adhere to the same evidence; the second investigation: objection by saying in accordance with the import of the verse; the third investigation: objection by claiming participation in the evidence; the fourth investigation: objection due to differing readings; the fifth investigation: objection by claiming abrogation; the sixth investigation: objection from the angle of interpretation; the seventh investigation: objection by counterargument. And in the second chapter, I dealt with jadal in inference from the Sunna (the objections raised against it and the answers to those objections), in four investigations: the first investigation: objections against inference from the Sunna from the angle of chain of transmission; the second investigation: objections raised against inference from the text (the substantive saying); the third investigation: objections against inference from the Sunna related to the cause; the fourth investigation: objections raised against inference from the actions of the Prophet, peace be upon him. As for the third chapter, I devoted it to discussing jadal in inference from qiyas (the objections raised against it) and I placed it in four investigations: the first investigation: objections raised against inference from qiyas from the angle of authoritativeness; the second investigation: objections raised against the generality of the pillars of qiyas; the third investigation: objections raised against the effective cause; the fourth investigation: objections raised against the correlational cause. Then follows the fourth and final chapter of the second section and the thesis together: Jadal in inference from the rest of the legal evidences, which consists of five investigations: the first investigation: jadal in inference from consensus; the second investigation: jadal in inference from the saying of a Companion; the third investigation: jadal in inference from presumption of continuity; the fourth investigation: jadal in inference from implication of the discourse (concept of agreement); the fifth investigation: jadal in inference from the directive of the discourse (concept of opposition). And after all that, I concluded this thesis with a conclusion containing the results that research led me to, as well as the horizons it opens for future scientific research. The difficulties that faced the research: In my research and investigation of the aspects, information, and elements of the subject, a number of difficulties confronted me, which can be summarized as follows: A - Difficulty in obtaining works of the usuliyyin specifically on jadal, because they are rare, little circulated, and not available in public libraries, and obtaining what is printed of them required me to wait a long time. B - Being the first to approach the subject in this manner—as far as I know and believe—made me like one entering a desolate forest with tangled branches without having a guide to show him the way or its landmarks, which made me change and modify the research plan many times, and I did not settle on a final plan until the last moments of the research. C - Scarcity of supporting references in public and private libraries made me resort often to photocopying, and what that entails of waiting, wasting time, and high material costs. D - The difficulty of the subject itself; it is a complex and far-reaching subject, and it was not easy to gather its elements in an integrated methodological form; the theoretical material was not available, and extracting it required long excursions in various books of usul al-fiqh, which are large encyclopedias with difficult language requiring a long background of experience and extended practice, and the applied material was scattered and dispersed, and collecting it similarly required long effort and great hardship.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is the author's introduction to his thesis on jadal (dialectical debate) among the usuliyyin (scholars of usul al-fiqh). The author first outlines the structure of the thesis, which is divided into two main sections (bab). The first section deals with the etiquettes (adab) of jadal, covering four investigations: the personality of the debater, the relationship between debaters, the subject of debate, and the style of debate. The second section covers applications of jadal to Islamic legal evidence, which he organizes around four types of evidence: the Quran (Book), the Sunna, qiyas (analogical deduction), and other legal evidences. For each type, he examines objections and answers. The chapter on the Book is subdivided into seven investigations addressing specific objections such as the arguer not adhering to the same evidence, the claim of abrogation, differing readings, etc. The chapter on the Sunna covers objections related to chain of transmission, text, cause, and actions of the Prophet. The chapter on qiyas examines objections regarding authoritativeness, pillars, effective cause, and correlational cause. The final chapter covers other evidences: consensus, saying of a Companion, presumption of continuity, implication of discourse (concept of agreement), and directive of discourse (concept of opposition). The author then describes difficulties encountered: scarcity of sources, being a pioneering work, lack of references, and the complexity of the subject matter.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Classifying Sources in Islamic Scholarship: Jadal as a Case Study
Author enumerates primary sources, general usul works, dictionaries, and biographical references.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize sources on jadal into primary specialized works, general usul works, lexicographical works, biographical works, and tafsir/hadith works, and explain the purpose of each category in research.

Core Idea
Source classification is the foundation of rigorous Islamic research.

This lesson uses the author's own classification of sources for his thesis on jadal among usuliyyun. By studying how he organizes his references, you will learn to distinguish between different types of scholarly works and understand their roles in building a research argument. This skill is essential for any researcher in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five categories
Read the passage and list the five distinct categories of sources the author mentions: specialized jadal books, general usul works, dictionaries, biographical works/fihrist, and tafsir/hadith works. Note that he also mentions contemporary studies as a sixth informal category.
Categories overlap in purpose but not in genre.
2
Distinguish specialized vs. general usul works
The author distinguishes books devoted entirely to jadal (e.g., al-Shirazi's al-Mu'wana) from general usul works that cover jadal as a topic (e.g., al-Baqillani's al-Taqrib). Compare the two lists and note that specialized works are few and limited.
Primary sources are the foundation; general works provide context.
3
Assess the author's benefit from each category
The author says benefit from specialized works was 'mutable'—some helped theoretically, others practically. From general usul works, benefit was 'jamma' (abundant). Consider why a researcher might need both.
Theoretical vs. practical utility is a recurring theme in source evaluation.
4
Recognize the role of auxiliary sources
Dictionaries (e.g., Lisan al-'Arab) define terms like jadal; biographical works (e.g., Siyar A'lam al-Nubala') help discover lost or manuscript works; tafsir works (e.g., Tahrir wa Tanwir) ground the study in revelation. Each serves a distinct purpose.
A holistic research approach integrates these auxiliary sources.
5
Synthesize the interdisciplinary method
The author states he even used works outside his field, arguing that sciences share principles. Reflect on how this openness enriches jadal studies. This step is about the 'why' behind the classification.
Interdisciplinarity is a modern research value, but it has roots in Islamic scholarship.

كلمة عن مصادر البحث ومراجعه : ولابد قبل أن أنهي هذه المقدمة كلمة عن المصادر والمراجع التي استمددت منها مادة هذه الرسالة العلمية . وأول ما يأتي في قائمة مصادر هذا الموضوع ؛ الكتب التي ألفها الأصوليون في الجدل، وهي قليلة ومحدودة: «المعونة في الجدل » للشيرازي، «المنهاج في ترتيب الحجاج، للباجي، الكافية في الجدل للجويني، «الجدل على طريقة الفقهاء لابن عقيل، وكذلك الجزئين الثاني والثالث من الواضح في أصول «الفقه لابن عقيل أيضًا، ثم علم الجذل في علم الجدل»، للطوفي، وهذه المؤلفات استفدت منها استفادة كبيرة لأنها المصادر الأساس للبحث، لكن الاستفادة منها متفاوتة، فقد استفدت من بعضها في الناحية النظرية أكثر، ومن البعض الآخر في الناحية التطبيقية أكثر . يلي بعد ذلك النوع الثاني من المصادر، وهو المؤلفات الأصولية العامة، وهذه استفدت منها كذلك استفادة جمة وأذكر منها على وجه الخصوص : «التقريب والإرشاد للباقلاني شرح اللمع الشيرازي البرهان في أصول الفقه الإمام الحرمين الجويني، إحكام الفصول في أحكام الأصول»، للباجي، «المحصول في علم الأصول» للرازي، العدة في أصول الفقه لأبي يعلى الفراء، «الواضح في أصول الفقه لابن عقيل، الإحكام في أصول الأحكام للآمدي، «روضة الناظر وجنة المناظر ، لابن قدامة، شرح مختصر «الروضة» للطوفي، «المغني في أصول الفقه الجلال الدين الخبازي، مفتاح الوصول إلى بناء الفروع على الأصول» للتلمساني، الإبهاج في شرح المنهاج للسبكي، و«البحر المحيط في أصول الفقه للزركشي، وغيرها كثير. كما رجعت في تحديد معنى الجدل وبعض المصطلحات الأخرى في اللغة العربية إلى عدد من المعاجم المعتمدة ومنها القاموس المحيط» للفيروز أبادي، لسان العرب لابن منظور، معجم مقاييس اللغة لابن فارس، «تهذيب اللغة» للأزهري، «مفردات ألفاظ القرآن للراغب الأصفهاني، وغيرها. وقد أعانتني كتب التراجم وفهارس الكتب، ومنها خاصة «سير أعلام النبلاء» للذهبي، وكشف الظنون لحاجي خليفة على معرفة الكثير من عناوين الكتب المؤلفة في الجدل والتي فقدت ولم تصل إلينا أو ما تزال مخطوطة لم تصل إليها يد التحقيق والطباعة . ورجعت في دراسة الجدل في القرآن والسنة إلى كتب التفسير والحديث، ومنها «تفسير التحرير والتنوير للشيخ محمد الطاهر بن عاشور، واستفدت من الدراسات الحديثة التي ظهرت في موضوع الجدل في القرآن الكريم خاصة، ومنها مثلا : مناهج الجدل في القرآن الكريم الزاهر عوض الألمعي، والجدل في القرآن الكريم للدكتور محمد ولم أستنكف عن الاستفادة من المؤلفات والبحوث المعاصرة التي وجدت فيها مادة لها صلة بالبحث حتى وإن كان يبدو أن هذه المؤلفات أو البحوث لا علاقة لها بمجال الدراسة، وذلك لأن العلوم وإن تباينت مجالاتها إلا أنها قد تتفق أحيانًا في بعض مسائلها أو في بعض أسس مناهجها.
A word about the sources of research and its references: Before I conclude this introduction, I must say a word about the sources and references from which I derived the material of this thesis. The first in the list of sources for this subject are the books authored by the usuliyyun on jadal, which are few and limited: 'al-Mu'wana fi al-Jadal' by al-Shirazi, 'al-Minhaj fi Tartib al-Hijaj' by al-Baji, 'al-Kafiya fi al-Jadal' by al-Juwayni, 'al-Jadal 'ala Tariqat al-Fuqaha'' by Ibn 'Aqil, as well as the second and third parts of 'al-Wadih fi Usul al-Fiqh' also by Ibn 'Aqil, then 'ʿIlm al-Jadhal fi ʿIlm al-Jadal' by al-Tufi. I benefited greatly from these works because they are the primary sources for the research, but the benefit from them varies; I benefited from some more in theoretical aspects and from others more in practical aspects. After that follows the second type of sources, which are general usul works, and I also benefited greatly from them. I mention in particular: 'al-Taqrib wa al-Irshad' by al-Baqillani, 'Sharh al-Luma'' by al-Shirazi, 'al-Burhan fi Usul al-Fiqh' by Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, 'Ihkam al-Fusul fi Ahkam al-Usul' by al-Baji, 'al-Mahsul fi ʿIlm al-Usul' by al-Razi, 'al-ʿUddah fi Usul al-Fiqh' by Abu Yaʿla al-Farra', 'al-Wadih fi Usul al-Fiqh' by Ibn ʿAqil, 'al-Ihkam fi Usul al-Ahkam' by al-Amidi, 'Rawdat al-Nazir wa Jannat al-Munazir' by Ibn Qudama, 'Sharh Mukhtasar al-Rawdah' by al-Tufi, 'al-Mughni fi Usul al-Fiqh' by Jalal al-Din al-Khabbazi, 'Miftah al-Wusul ila Bina' al-Furu' 'ala al-Usul' by al-Tilimsani, 'al-Ibhaj fi Sharh al-Minhaj' by al-Subki, and 'al-Bahr al-Muhit fi Usul al-Fiqh' by al-Zarkashi, and many others. I also consulted for defining the meaning of jadal and some other terms in the Arabic language a number of reliable dictionaries, including 'al-Qamus al-Muhit' by al-Fayruzabadi, 'Lisan al-ʿArab' by Ibn Manzur, 'Mu'jam Maqayis al-Lugha' by Ibn Faris, 'Tahdhib al-Lugha' by al-Azhari, 'Mufradat Alfaz al-Qur'an' by al-Raghib al-Isfahani, and others. Biographical works and book indexes also helped me, especially 'Siyar A'lam al-Nubala'' by al-Dhahabi, and 'Kashf al-Zunun' by Haji Khalifa, in knowing many titles of books authored on jadal that have been lost and have not reached us, or that are still in manuscript form and have not been subjected to editing and printing. I also referred to books of tafsir and hadith in studying jadal in the Qur'an and Sunnah, including 'Tafsir al-Tahrir wa al-Tanwir' by Shaykh Muhammad al-Tahir ibn ʿAshur, and I benefited from modern studies that appeared on the subject of jadal in the Holy Qur'an in particular, such as 'Manahij al-Jadal fi al-Qur'an al-Karim' by Zahir ʿAwad al-Almaʿi, and 'al-Jadal fi al-Qur'an al-Karim' by Dr. Muhammad... And I did not disdain from benefiting from contemporary works and research in which I found material related to the research, even if it seemed that these works or research had no connection to the field of study, because the sciences, though their fields differ, may sometimes agree in some of their issues or some of the foundations of their methodologies.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a detailed listing of sources by the author of a modern academic thesis on jadal (dialectical disputation) among usuliyyun (scholars of usul al-fiqh). The author categorizes his sources into several types. First, he mentions specialized works on jadal by usuliyyun, which are few and limited, such as those by al-Shirazi, al-Baji, al-Juwayni, Ibn 'Aqil, and al-Tufi. He notes that although these are primary sources, his benefit from them varied—some were more useful theoretically, others practically. Second, he lists general usul al-fiqh works that also contributed significantly, including classics like al-Baqillani's 'al-Taqrib wa al-Irshad', al-Juwayni's 'al-Burhan', al-Razi's 'al-Mahsul', and many others. Third, he consulted Arabic dictionaries such as 'al-Qamus al-Muhit' and 'Lisan al-'Arab' for defining terms. Fourth, biographical works like 'Siyar A'lam al-Nubala'' and 'Kashf al-Zunun' helped identify lost or unpublished books on jadal. Fifth, he used tafsir and hadith works, notably 'Tafsir al-Tahrir wa al-Tanwir', and modern studies on jadal in the Qur'an. Finally, he acknowledges benefiting from contemporary research even outside his immediate field, arguing that sciences may share principles. The passage demonstrates the author's comprehensive and interdisciplinary approach to sourcing.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Methodological Rigour in Jadal Research
Author describes his methodology, sources, and thesis structure in the preface.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key methodological elements (takhrij, tarjama, documentation) used by a contemporary Islamic scholar in a study on jadal.

Core Idea
Scholarly rigor in Islamic research requires precise source referencing, biographical verification, and clear documentation.

This preface from 'Jadal Usuliyyin' reveals the meticulous method a scholar employs to ensure accuracy. Even in a work on dialectic, the foundation is sound source work. Understanding these elements helps you evaluate the credibility of any Islamic academic work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main methodological components
Scan the passage for the list of elements (a to g). Note how the author prioritises accurate Quranic and hadith writing, takhrij, tarjama, direct citation, and indexing.
These elements are common in contemporary Islamic research (tahqiq).
2
Understand takhrij and tarjama
Takhrij means referencing verses and hadiths to their sources. Tarjama means providing a biographical sketch. Observe that the author uses the Sakhr CD for hadith numbering and relies on ancient sources for biographies.
Takhrij ensures traceability; tarjama establishes the credibility of sources.
3
Analyse the balance between primary and secondary sources
The author uses primary works (tafsir, hadith, classical bibliographies) but also modern studies. He justifies using disparate sciences if they share methodological foundations.
This shows openness to beneficial modern tools without abandoning traditional methods.
4
Reflect on the importance of methodological transparency
The author lists his indexes and his commitment to clarity. This transparency allows readers to verify claims and follow the research path.
Such transparency is a hallmark of trustworthy Islamic scholarship.

The debate [book] for al-Dhahabi, and Kashf al-Zunun by Hajji Khalifa, for knowing many titles of books authored on jadal (dialectic) which were lost and did not reach us, or are still manuscripts that have not been reached by verification and printing. I referred in studying jadal in the Quran and Sunnah to books of tafsir and hadith. Among them is 'Tafsir al-Tahrir wa al-Tanwir' by Sheikh Muhammad al-Tahir ibn Ashur, and I benefited from modern studies that appeared on the topic of jadal in the Noble Quran especially, including for example: 'Manahij al-Jadal fi al-Quran al-Karim' by al-Zahir 'Awad al-Alma'i, and 'al-Jadal fi al-Quran al-Karim' by Dr. Muhammad. I did not disdain from benefiting from contemporary works and research in which I found material related to the study, even if it appears that these works or research have no relation to the field of study, because sciences, though their fields differ, may sometimes agree in some of their issues or in some foundations of their methodologies. The method adopted in writing the thesis: At the conclusion of this introduction, I wish to indicate that in writing the thesis I adopted a method based essentially on a number of essential elements, namely: a- Care for the correct writing of Quranic verses, with diacritics, and likewise the takhrij (referencing) of these verses by citing their positions in the surahs of the Noble Quran. b- Likewise care for the correct writing of noble prophetic hadiths, and their takhrij by citing their positions in books of Sunnah, adopting the numbering of the international edition of the nine books of hadith, using the compact disc produced by experts of Sakhr software company, which gained the satisfaction and trust of scholars and researchers. c- Biographical translation for most of the prominent figures mentioned in the text of the thesis by referring to authentic sources in this field, and I did not refer to modern references except when I was unable to find the biography in ancient sources, and I did not omit the biography of anyone I mentioned except for the well-known, such as the Companions and some Followers. d- My concern was intense for correct scholarly documentation of the information I transmitted directly from original sources of research without using indirect transmission except in very rare cases which I indicated in their places in the research, for sources I did not find. I committed when citing a source or reference for the first time to mention all information pertaining to it, and I did not neglect this except regarding biographical sources, where I sufficed with mentioning the part and page number due to the impossibility of transmitting all information about each source, contenting myself with mentioning this information in the list of sources and references at the end of the thesis. And I appended the thesis with a number of detailed indexes that would help its reader reach their desired goal by the easiest way, and these indexes relate to Quranic verses, prophetic hadiths, poems and defined terms, principles and usuli issues and fiqh issues, prominent figures, books mentioned in the text, then the list of sources and references, and finally an index of topics. And I adorned the chapters and sections of the thesis with a scholarly style that aims at clarity and exposition as much as possible, and completely avoided the style of verbosity and verbal ostentation, out of concern for the necessary scholarly accuracy in any research that deals with a scientific issue and seeks to reach results that serve knowledge and contribute to its enrichment. I hope that I have succeeded in treating the topic as it deserves of research, presentation, and analysis, and that I have contributed in adding a new brick to the edifice of the science of usul al-fiqh and its methodologies of study and research. My success is only from Allah; He is my sufficiency, upon Him I rely, and to Him I turn in repentance. Praise be to Him in the first and the last. The one in need of his Lord's forgiveness, Mas'ud bin Musa Falusi al-Ghasiri, Tazawalt - Batna, Friday 5 Jumada al-Thani 1422 AH, corresponding to 24 August 2001 CE.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is the preface of the book 'Jadal Usuliyyin', in which the author explains his methodology and sources. He begins by noting his use of earlier bibliographic works like 'al-Jadal' by al-Dhahabi and 'Kashf al-Zunun' to survey lost works on dialectic. He then states that for studying jadal in the Quran and Sunnah, he relied on tafsir and hadith works, especially 'Tafsir al-Tahrir wa al-Tanwir' by Ibn Ashur, and modern studies on Quranic jadal. He acknowledges benefiting from contemporary research even outside his field, reasoning that different sciences may share common issues or methodological foundations. The author then outlines his methodological elements: (a) accurate writing and referencing (takhrij) of Quranic verses with diacritics; (b) accurate writing and referencing of hadiths using the standard international edition of the nine hadith books via the Sakhr software CD; (c) providing biographical notes (tarjama) for most figures mentioned, relying on ancient sources except when unavailable; (d) meticulous documentation by citing original sources directly, avoiding indirect transmission except rarely; (e) full citation details on first mention except for biographical sources where only volume and page are given, with full details in the bibliography; (f) including detailed indexes for verses, hadiths, poetry, terms, usuli and fiqhi issues, figures, books, sources, and topics; (g) adopting a clear, scholarly style free from verbosity. The author closes with a prayer for success and a note on his location and date. This preface reveals the author's academic rigor and his commitment to primary sources, which is crucial for a work on the methodology of jadal in usul al-fiqh.

ترجمة
الجدل

Methodological Rigor in Islamic Research
Author's methodological preface.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of a rigorous research methodology as described by this author.

Core Idea
Methodological transparency is the backbone of credible Islamic scholarship.

This passage comes from the author's preface, where he outlines his approach to researching a topic in usul al-fiqh. He emphasizes reliance on original sources, clear referencing, and comprehensive indices. Understanding his methodology helps us appreciate the standards required for serious Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Locate the author's principle of source use
Find the statement about using original sources directly versus intermediaries. Note the rare exceptions he mentions.
He says 'مباشرة من المصادر الأصيلة' (directly from original sources) and only rarely via intermediaries.
2
Examine his referencing method
Identify how he treats first-time references and biographical sources. What information is given fully vs. abbreviated?
For first references, full info; for biographical sources, only volume and page due to difficulty.
3
Analyze the structure of his indices
List the types of indices he includes. Why are they important for the reader?
Indices for Quran, hadith, poems, terms, usul/fiqh issues, notable persons, books, and subjects.
4

What does he say about his writing style? What does he avoid? Why?
He aims for clarity (الوضوح والبيان) and avoids padding and verbal showiness for scientific precision.

Commentary
This passage is the author's preface, where he outlines the methodology of his research. He emphasizes that he relied directly on original sources and rarely used intermediary transmissions, except for a few cases he notes. For first-time references, he provides full bibliographic information, but for biographical sources, he only gives volume and page due to the difficulty of conveying all details. He then lists the indices he included at the end of the thesis: for Quranic verses, hadiths, poems and terms, usul and fiqh rules and issues, notable persons, and books, followed by a list of sources and a subject index. He states that he wrote the chapters in a clear, scientific style, avoiding verbosity and showiness, to maintain precision. He expresses hope that his work contributes to the field of usul al-fiqh. Finally, he signs off with a prayer and date. This is standard front matter, not a logical argument.

Rationale for Studying Jadal's Roots
Author outlines the structure of the preliminary section on the roots of jadal and its development in usul al-fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author traces the roots of jadal before presenting its theory in usul al-fiqh, and identify the two main chapters of the preliminary section.

Core Idea
Jadal is rooted in human instinct and revelation, not a later innovation.

This preamble sets the stage for a rigorous study of jadal. Before diving into its applications in usul al-fiqh, the author insists on establishing its foundations. Understanding this structure helps you appreciate how Islamic scholars ground their methods in both innate human nature and divine sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's overall claim
Read the first sentence: the author argues that jadal has roots in human instinct and Islamic sources, which justifies its use in usul al-fiqh. This is a methodological stance.
2
List the three sources mentioned for tracing roots
Find: (1) human instinct, (2) Quran and Sunnah, (3) practices of companions, successors, and imams. These are the foundations the author will explore.
3
Distinguish the two chronological phases
The author separates roots before usul al-fiqh (Chapter One) from development within usul al-fiqh (Chapter Two). This shows a clear logical progression.
4
Recognize the purpose of this preliminary section
The author states that tracing roots 'justifies the establishment and existence' of jadal. So this preamble is about legitimacy, not just history.

توطئة : الباب التمهيدي جذور الجدل ونشأته وتطوره عند الأصوليين خضوعا لمنطق التراتب بين الأفكار المرتبط بعضها ببعض، نحتاج قبل عرض نظرية الجدل وتطبيقاته عند علماء أصول الفقه إلى تأصيل هذا المنهج وإبراز أصوله الشرعية التي تبرر قيامه ووجوده، وذلك بتتبع جذوره في الفطرة الإنسانية، ثم توظيفه في القرآن الكريم والسنة النبوية وكذا ظهوره وانتشاره كمنهج فكري عند كل من الصحابة والتابعين والأئمة المجتهدين . ثم نتناول بعد ذلك نشأة وتطور الجدل في أصول الفقه والمراحل التي مر بها وإسهامات الأصوليين في هذا الباب في كل مرحلة من تلك المراحل. لذلك، سيكون هذا الباب التمهيدي من الرسالة متكونا من فصلين : الفصل الأول : جذور الجدل قبل علم أصول الفقه . الفصل الثاني : نشأة وتطور البحث الجدلي في أصول الفقه . ***
Preamble: The Preliminary Section: Roots of Jadal, its Origin and Development among the Usuliyyin. Subject to the logic of the order among interconnected ideas, we need, before presenting the theory of jadal and its applications among the scholars of usul al-fiqh, to establish the foundations of this method and highlight its shar'i roots that justify its establishment and existence, by tracing its roots in human instinct, then its employment in the Noble Quran and Prophetic Sunnah, as well as its emergence and spread as an intellectual method among the companions, successors, and mujtahid imams. Then we will address thereafter the origin and development of jadal in usul al-fiqh, the stages it passed through, and the contributions of the usuliyyin in this field in each of those stages. Therefore, this preliminary section of the thesis will consist of two chapters: Chapter One: Roots of Jadal before the Science of Usul al-Fiqh. Chapter Two: Origin and Development of Dialectical Research in Usul al-Fiqh.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is the preamble to the first section of the book, where the author explains the rationale and structure of his preliminary investigation into jadal (dialectical argumentation). He argues that before presenting the theory and applications of jadal in usul al-fiqh, one must first establish its foundations by tracing its roots in human instinct, its use in the Quran and Sunnah, and its development among early Muslim generations. Then he will discuss its evolution within usul al-fiqh itself. The section is divided into two chapters: the first covers the roots of jadal before the formal science of usul al-fiqh, and the second covers the origin and development of jadal within usul al-fiqh. This is a methodological outline, not a logical argument.

الفطرة الإنسانية
الأصول الشرعية

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Jadal as an Authentic Islamic Method: An Overview
Author introduces the roots of jadal in human nature, Quran, Sunnah, and early practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to outline the author's plan for tracing the roots of jadal and identify the three primary justifications for jadal as an authentic method in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Jadal is not a foreign import; it is rooted in human nature, revelation, and early practice.

This lesson introduces the author's preliminary framework for studying jadal in usul al-fiqh. The author argues that jadal has deep Islamic roots, and he will systematically trace them from human nature to the Quran, Sunnah, and the practice of the early generations. Understanding this foundation is crucial before engaging with the logical and argumentative applications of jadal in later lessons.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's stated purpose
The author emphasizes the need to establish the roots of jadal before presenting its theory. What is his main reason?
Look for the phrase 'خضوعا لمنطق التراتب' (subject to the logic of ordering).
2
List the three sources of justification
The author mentions three justifications: innate human disposition (fitra), the Quran and Sunnah, and the practice of the salaf. Write them down.
These are found after 'يستمد مبرراته من كون الجدل نزعة فطرية...'
3
Note the defense against foreign origin
The author explicitly states jadal is 'not a method born from a vacuum or derived from the ideas of others'. This directly counters any claim of foreign influence.
This is a key rhetorical move in the passage.
4
Recognize the five sections of the chapter
The author lists five sections covering jadal in fitra, Quran, Sunnah, era of Companions & Followers, and era of Successors & Mujtahid Imams.
These correspond to 'خمسة مباحث'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خضوعا لمنطق التراتب بين الأفكار المرتبط بعضها ببعض، نحتاج قبل عرض نظرية الجدل وتطبيقاته عند علماء أصول الفقه إلى تأصيل هذا المنهج وإبراز أصوله الشرعية التي تبرر قيامه ووجوده، وذلك بتتبع جذوره في الفطرة الإنسانية، ثم توظيفه في القرآن الكريم والسنة النبوية وكذا ظهوره وانتشاره كمنهج فكري عند كل من الصحابة والتابعين والأئمة المجتهدين .
ثم نتناول بعد ذلك نشأة وتطور الجدل في أصول الفقه والمراحل التي مر بها وإسهامات الأصوليين في هذا الباب في كل مرحلة من تلك المراحل. لذلك، سيكون هذا الباب التمهيدي من الرسالة متكونا من فصلين :
الفصل الأول : جذور الجدل قبل علم أصول الفقه . الفصل الثاني : نشأة وتطور البحث الجدلي في أصول الفقه .

إن الجدل عند علماء أصول الفقه ليس منهجًا ناشتا من فراغ أو مستمدا أفكار الآخرين وإنما هو منهج أصيل يستمد مبرراته من كون الجدل نزعة فطرية في الإنسان أولا، ثم من أصول الإسلام في الكتاب والسنة التي اتخذت من الجدل وسيلة إلى تبليغ الدين والدفاع عن أصوله وفروعه، كما يستمد مبرراته أيضًا ممارسة الرعيل الأول من السلف الصالح له باعتباره أداة لتبليغ الدين والدفاع عنه، وكذا وسيلة لإدارة وجوه النظر في الاجتهادات التي يتوصلون إليها بغية التحقق من سلامتها وصحة ما يتم التوصل إليه بشأنها. وعلى هذا الأساس أحاول في هذا الفصل التأصيل لمنهج الجدل عند علماء أصول الفقه بتتبع هذا المنهج في أصوله الأولى في النفس الإنسانية، ثم باستخراج مبررات الجدل من القرآن الكريم الذي استعمل هذا المنهج، ثم من السنة النبوية الشريفة التي استعملت هذا المنهج أيضًا، ثم أنتقل بعد ذلك إلى تأصيل هذا المنهج في عمل الصحابة والتابعين والأئمة المجتهدين.
لذلك سيتكون هذا الفصل من خمسة مباحث، هي: المبحث الأول : الجدل منزع فطري في الإنسان .
المبحث الثاني: الجدل في القرآن الكريم. المبحث الثالث: الجدل في السنة النبوية الشريفة . المبحث الرابع: الجدل في عصر الصحابة والتابعين .
المبحث الخامس : الجدل في عصر أتباع التابعين والأئمة المجتهدين
Subject to the logic of the ordering of ideas that are interconnected, we need, before presenting the theory of jadal and its applications among the scholars of usul al-fiqh, to establish the foundations of this method and highlight its legal origins that justify its establishment and existence, by tracing its roots in human nature (fitra), then its employment in the Noble Quran and Prophetic Sunnah, as well as its emergence and spread as an intellectual method among the Companions, Followers, and the Mujtahid Imams.
Then we will discuss after that the emergence and development of jadal in usul al-fiqh, the stages it went through, and the contributions of the usul scholars in this subject at each of those stages. Therefore, this preliminary chapter of the treatise will consist of two chapters:
Chapter One: The roots of jadal before the science of usul al-fiqh. Chapter Two: The emergence and development of jadal research in usul al-fiqh.

Indeed, jadal among the scholars of usul al-fiqh is not a method born from a vacuum or derived from the ideas of others, but rather it is an authentic method that derives its justifications from the fact that jadal is an innate disposition (naz'a fitriyya) in humans first, then from the foundations of Islam in the Book and Sunnah which took jadal as a means to convey the religion and defend its principles and branches, and it also derives its justifications from the practice of the first generation of the pious predecessors (al-salaf al-salih) of it as a tool for conveying the religion and defending it, as well as a means for managing the aspects of consideration in the ijtihads they arrived at in order to verify their soundness and the correctness of what is concluded regarding them. On this basis, I attempt in this chapter to establish the foundations of the method of jadal among the scholars of usul al-fiqh by tracing this method in its first origins in the human soul, then by extracting the justifications of jadal from the Noble Quran which used this method, then from the Noble Prophetic Sunnah which also used this method, then I move after that to establishing the foundations of this method in the practice of the Companions, Followers, and the Mujtahid Imams.
Therefore, this chapter will consist of five sections: First Section: Jadal as an innate disposition in humans. Second Section: Jadal in the Noble Quran. Third Section: Jadal in the Noble Prophetic Sunnah. Fourth Section: Jadal in the era of the Companions and Followers. Fifth Section: Jadal in the era of the Successors of the Followers and the Mujtahid Imams.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is the preface to the preliminary chapter of a study on jadal (dialectic) among usul al-fiqh scholars. The author begins by stating the need, due to the logical ordering of interconnected ideas, to first establish the foundations of the jadal method and highlight its religious origins before presenting its theory and applications. He proposes to trace its roots in human nature (fitra), its use in the Quran and Sunnah, and its emergence as an intellectual method among the early generations. Then he outlines the two-chapter structure of the preliminary chapter: the first on the roots of jadal before usul al-fiqh, and the second on its development within usul al-fiqh.

On page 19, the author elaborates that jadal is not a foreign or borrowed method but an authentic one, justified by its being an innate human disposition, by the Islamic sources (Quran and Sunnah) using it as a means for conveying and defending the religion, and by the practice of the early righteous generations (salaf) who used it for the same purposes and for verifying ijtihads. Based on this, the author intends to trace jadal's origins in the human soul, then extract its justifications from the Quran and Sunnah, and finally ground it in the practice of the Companions, Followers, and Mujtahid Imams. The chapter will then comprise five sections: (1) jadal as an innate disposition, (2) jadal in the Quran, (3) jadal in the Sunnah, (4) jadal in the era of the Companions and Followers, and (5) jadal in the era of the Successors of the Followers and the Mujtahid Imams.

تأصيل
الفطرة الإنسانية
أصول الفقه

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
7 min
The Three Roots of Jadal in Islamic Tradition
Author expands on the three sources of justification for jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how jadal is justified by (1) human fitra, (2) the Quran and Sunnah, and (3) the practice of the early Muslim generations.

Core Idea
The Islamic legitimacy of jadal rests on three pillars: innate human disposition, divine revelation, and prophetic and early practice.

In this lesson, we dig deeper into the three justificatory pillars the author proposes. The author argues that jadal is not merely a logical tool adopted from external sources, but is part of human nature (fitra), was employed by Allah and His Prophet in the Quran and Sunnah, and was actively used by the Companions and early scholars to verify and defend religious knowledge. Recognizing these roots gives us confidence in the method's Islamic authenticity.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first pillar: Human fitra
The author says jadal is an innate disposition (naz'a fitriyya). Reflect on how debate and argumentation are natural human tendencies. Why does this provide a universal basis for jadal?
Consider the Islamic concept of fitra as the original state of creation.
2
Examine the second pillar: The Quran and Sunnah
Consider that the Quran itself contains many dialectical passages (e.g., debates with polytheists). How does the use of jadal in revelation legitimize its use in scholarship?
Look for the phrase 'اتخذت من الجدل وسيلة إلى تبليغ الدين' (took jadal as a means to convey the religion).
3
Examine the third pillar: Practice of the salaf
The early generations used jadal to convey, defend, and verify ijtihads. How does their practice give authority to the method, especially for fiqh discussions?
The author notes that they used it 'لإدارة وجوه النظر في الاجتهادات' (managing aspects of consideration in ijtihads).
4
Observe the author's methodology
The author uses these three pillars to argue that jadal is authentic and not borrowed. This is an 'expansion' from the general claim to specific evidences.
This method is typical in Islamic scholarship: arguing from fitra, naql (revelation), and 'aql (reason/precedent).

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
10 min
The Authentic Roots of Jadal in Usul al-Fiqh
Author introduces the innate origin of jadal and its roots in Quran, Sunnah, and early generations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three sources of legitimacy for jadal (dialectic) as presented in this passage: human fitrah, Quran and Sunnah, and the practice of the salaf.

Core Idea
Jadal is not an imported methodology but an authentic Islamic tool rooted in creation and revelation.

This opening passage sets the stage for the entire chapter. The author, a scholar of usul al-fiqh, argues that jadal (constructive debate) is not a borrowed methodology from Greek logic or other traditions. Instead, it is deeply rooted in three fundamental sources: the innate human disposition (fitrah), the primary texts of Islam (Quran and Sunnah), and the practice of the early generations (salaf). Understanding these roots is essential because it establishes jadal as a legitimate and necessary tool for ijtihad and defending the faith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thesis
The author states outright that jadal is an authentic methodology (manhaj asil) not derived from others. Find this assertion.
Look for the phrase 'ليس منهجا ناشتا من فراغ أو مستمدا أفكار الآخرين'.
2
List the three justifications
The passage explicitly mentions three sources: (1) jadal as an innate disposition (fitrah), (2) its use in Quran and Sunnah, (3) the practice of the salaf. Extract them.
The sources are introduced with 'من كون الجدل نزعة فطرية... ثم من أصول الإسلام... ثم ممارسة الرعيل الأول'.
3
Notice the chapter structure
The author then announces the chapter will have five sections. The first section elaborates on fitrah. The remaining sections correspond to the other sources.
The five sections are listed after 'سيتكون هذا الفصل من خمسة مباحث'.
4
Explore the fitrah argument
The passage explains that humans are created with contemplation (ta'ammul) and speech (nutq), and diversity leads to intellectual difference. This is the natural basis for jadal.
Key terms: التأمل والتفكير, النطق, التنوع.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
توطئة : الفصل الأول جذور الجدل قبل علم أصول الفقه إن الجدل عند علماء أصول الفقه ليس منهجًا ناشتا من فراغ أو مستمدا أفكار الآخرين وإنما هو منهج أصيل يستمد مبرراته من كون الجدل نزعة فطرية في الإنسان أولا، ثم من أصول الإسلام في الكتاب والسنة التي اتخذت من الجدل وسيلة إلى تبليغ الدين والدفاع عن أصوله وفروعه، كما يستمد مبرراته أيضًا ممارسة الرعيل الأول من السلف الصالح له باعتباره أداة لتبليغ الدين والدفاع عنه، وكذا وسيلة لإدارة وجوه النظر في الاجتهادات التي يتوصلون إليها بغية التحقق من سلامتها وصحة ما يتم التوصل إليه بشأنها. وعلى هذا الأساس أحاول في هذا الفصل التأصيل لمنهج الجدل عند علماء أصول الفقه بتتبع هذا المنهج في أصوله الأولى في النفس الإنسانية، ثم باستخراج مبررات الجدل من القرآن الكريم الذي استعمل هذا المنهج، ثم من السنة النبوية الشريفة التي استعملت هذا المنهج أيضًا، ثم أنتقل بعد ذلك إلى تأصيل هذا المنهج في عمل الصحابة والتابعين والأئمة المجتهدين. لذلك سيتكون هذا الفصل من خمسة مباحث، هي: المبحث الأول : الجدل منزع فطري في الإنسان . المبحث الثاني: الجدل في القرآن الكريم. المبحث الثالث: الجدل في السنة النبوية الشريفة . المبحث الرابع: الجدل في عصر الصحابة والتابعين . المبحث الخامس : الجدل في عصر أتباع التابعين والأئمة المجتهدين المبحث الأول الجدل منزع فطري في الإنسان المطلب الأول : الجدل ظاهرة كونية : خلق الله عز وجل الإنسان، وفطره على القدرة على التأمل والتفكير، الذي هو نتيجة للدهشة والتساؤل اللذين ينتابان ذهن الإنسان حين مشاهدة ما يحيط به في هذا الكون من مخلوقات مختلفة الأنواع والأشكال والوظائف، وكذا محاولته فهم ذاته وكنه وجوده والعلاقة التي تربطه بالكون وما فيه وكما فطر الله سبحانه وتعالى الإنسان على التساؤل والتفكير، فقد فطره كذلك على النطق بما ينتهي إليه تأمله وتفكيره، إذ يجد نفسه مدفوعا بالجبلة والطبع إلى حب الإفضاء بأفكاره ومعلوماته والإفصاح عنها وإذا كانت هذه حقيقة فطرية ملموسة، فإن هناك حقيقة فطرية أخرى لا يختلف حولها اثنان وتلك هي أن التنوع حقيقة إنسانية تبدأ من الاختلاف في التكوين العضوي بين البشر - ذكر وأنثى - وتنتهي بعشرات الصور التي تعكس هذا الاختلاف والتنوع - في الألوان والطبائع والأمزجة وأنماط الحياة والسلوك .. الخ - وهذا التنوع هو الميزة الأرقى لهذا الكائن البشري، وهو النتيجة الطبيعية لعناصر الحرية والاختيار اللذين يتميز بهما الكائن البشري ، وهذا التنوع في هذه المستويات كلها، يفضي - بالطبيعة - إلى تنوع ما يصل إليه الإنسان بتأمله وتفكيره . . لذلك كان الاختلاف الفكري بين بني الإنسان قديما قدم الإنسان في هذه الأرض، ابتدأ حيث ابتدأ ينظر إلى الكون فيشده بعظمته، وتأخذه الحيرة في إدراك كنهه وحقيقته
Preface: Chapter One: The Roots of Dialectic before the Science of Usul al-Fiqh. Indeed, dialectic according to scholars of usul al-fiqh is not a methodology that came from nothing or borrowed the ideas of others; rather it is an authentic methodology that derives its justification from the fact that dialectic is an innate disposition in humans first, then from the foundations of Islam in the Book and the Sunnah, which took dialectic as a means to convey religion and defend its principles and branches. Likewise, it also derives its justification from the practice of the first generation of the righteous predecessors in using it as a tool for conveying and defending religion, as well as a means for managing aspects of consideration in the ijtihads they arrive at, in order to verify their soundness and correctness. On this basis, I attempt in this chapter to establish the roots of the methodology of dialectic among scholars of usul al-fiqh by tracing this methodology in its first roots in the human soul, then by extracting the justifications for dialectic from the noble Qur'an which used this methodology, then from the noble Prophetic Sunnah which also used this methodology, then I move on to establishing the roots of this methodology in the practice of the Companions, Successors, and the mujtahid imams. Therefore, this chapter will consist of five sections: The first section: Dialectic as an innate disposition in humans. The second section: Dialectic in the noble Qur'an. The third section: Dialectic in the noble Prophetic Sunnah. The fourth section: Dialectic in the era of the Companions and Successors. The fifth section: Dialectic in the era of the Followers of the Successors and the mujtahid imams. The first section: Dialectic as an innate disposition in humans. The first part: Dialectic as a universal phenomenon: Allah, the Mighty and Sublime, created the human being, and instilled in him - among what He instilled - the ability for contemplation and thought, which is the result of wonder and questioning that overtake the human mind when observing the creations around him in this universe of various types, forms, and functions, as well as his attempt to understand himself, the essence of his existence, and the relationship that connects him to the universe and what is in it. And just as Allah, the Exalted, instilled in humans questioning and thought, He also instilled in them speech to express what their contemplation and thinking lead to, for he finds himself driven by nature and disposition to love expressing his thoughts and information and disclosing them. If this is a tangible innate fact, there is another innate fact about which no two disagree: that diversity is a human reality that begins with differences in organic composition among humans - male and female - and ends with dozens of images that reflect this difference and diversity - in colors, temperaments, dispositions, lifestyles, behavior, etc. This diversity is the highest characteristic of this human being, and it is the natural result of the elements of freedom and choice that distinguish the human being. This diversity at all these levels naturally leads to diversity in what humans arrive at through their contemplation and thought. Therefore, intellectual difference among humans is as old as humans on this earth, beginning when they began to look at the universe and were struck by its greatness, and were seized by confusion in comprehending its essence and reality.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to the first chapter of the book, establishing the origins of dialectic (jadal) in Islamic jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh). The author begins by asserting that jadal among usul scholars is not an imported or borrowed methodology but an authentic one rooted in three sources: (1) the innate human disposition, (2) the Islamic sources of Quran and Sunnah which used jadal as a means of conveying and defending the religion, and (3) the practice of the early pious predecessors (salaf) who used it as a tool for ijtihad and verification. Then the author outlines the plan of the chapter, which contains five sections: the innate disposition, the Qur'an, the Sunnah, the era of the Companions and Successors, and the era of the Followers and mujtahid imams. After this introduction, the first section begins discussing jadal as a universal human phenomenon. It explains that Allah created humans with the ability to contemplate and think, sparked by wonder and questioning when observing the diversity of creation. Moreover, humans are naturally inclined to express their thoughts through speech. The passage then highlights a second innate fact: human diversity—biological, psychological, and behavioral—which naturally leads to diversity of thought and thus to intellectual differences. This difference is as old as humanity itself, originating from humans' awe before the universe and their confusion in understanding its reality. The commentary follows the author's order: first the overall justification of jadal, then the chapter outline, then the specific discussion of the innate foundations.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Jadal is an Innate Human Trait
Author argues that jadal is an innate universal human phenomenon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why jadal is considered an innate (fitri) and universal human phenomenon based on human nature and diversity.

Core Idea
Jadal is not a skill we learn; it is the natural expression of our fitra.

This passage establishes the foundation for studying jadal in Islamic logic. It argues that debate is not a foreign or negative activity, but a natural outcome of how God created humans—with the capacity to think, question, and express. Understanding this helps us see jadal as part of the human condition, not a specialized art only for scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three fitri capacities mentioned
The author lists thinking (tafakkur), speech (nutq), and the drive to interact as innate capacities. Note that all are part of fitra.
2
Trace how diversity leads to debate
Physical and intellectual diversity, combined with interaction, creates difference of opinion, which naturally leads to dialogue and argument.
3
Observe the use of Quran 49:13
The author uses the verse to show that interaction and acquaintance are part of God's plan, and this inevitably involves exchange of ideas.
4
Extend the argument to children
The author gives an example of children defending their rights, showing jadal is present from early age.
5
Formulate the conclusion in your own words
Jadal is a natural, inescapable human trait. Summarize the author's reasoning in one sentence.

المبحث الأول
الجدل منزع فطري في الإنسان
المطلب الأول : الجدل ظاهرة كونية :
خلق الله عز وجل الإنسان، وفطره - فيما فطره - على القدرة على التأمل والتفكير، الذي هو نتيجة للدهشة والتساؤل اللذين ينتابان ذهن الإنسان حين مشاهدة ما يحيط به في هذا الكون من مخلوقات مختلفة الأنواع والأشكال والوظائف، وكذا محاولته فهم ذاته وكنه وجوده والعلاقة التي تربطه بالكون وما فيه. وكما فطر الله سبحانه وتعالى الإنسان على التساؤل والتفكير، فقد فطره كذلك على النطق بما ينتهي إليه تأمله وتفكيره، إذ يجد نفسه مدفوعا بالجبلة والطبع إلى حب الإفضاء بأفكاره ومعلوماته والإفصاح عنها. وإذا كانت هذه حقيقة فطرية ملموسة، فإن هناك حقيقة فطرية أخرى لا يختلف حولها اثنان وتلك هي أن التنوع حقيقة إنسانية تبدأ من الاختلاف في التكوين العضوي بين البشر - ذكر وأنثى - وتنتهي بعشرات الصور التي تعكس هذا الاختلاف والتنوع - في الألوان والطبائع والأمزجة وأنماط الحياة والسلوك .. الخ - وهذا التنوع هو الميزة الأرقى لهذا الكائن البشري، وهو النتيجة الطبيعية لعناصر الحرية والاختيار اللذين يتميز بهما الكائن البشري، وهذا التنوع في هذه المستويات كلها، يفضي - بالطبيعة - إلى تنوع ما يصل إليه الإنسان بتأمله وتفكيره . . لذلك كان الاختلاف الفكري بين بني الإنسان قديما قدم الإنسان في هذه الأرض، ابتدأ حيث ابتدأ ينظر إلى الكون فيشده بعظمته، وتأخذه الحيرة في إدراك كنهه وحقيقته. ثم إن الإنسان إلى جانب كونه مفطورًا على التنوع والاختلاف الفكري، هو مفطور كذلك على التفاعل مع غيره من بني جنسه بواسطة الاحتكاك والتعارف كما قال تعالى : (يَتأَيّها النَّاسُ إِنَّا خَلَقْتَكُم مِّن ذَكَرٍ وَأُنثَى وَجَعَلْتَكُمْ شُعُوبا وقبائل لتعارفوا إِنَّ أَكْرَمَكُمْ عِندَ اللهِ أَنقَلَكُم إِنَّ اللهَ عَلِيمٌ خَيْرٌ). ومن الطبيعي أن ينتج هذا التفاعل التقاء الأفكار وتصادمها وتنازعها، إذ يجد كل إنسان نفسه مدفوعا إلى التعبير عن خواطره وأفكاره والإفصاح عنها وعرضها للحوار والجدل فينشأ إثر ذلك الحجاج والنقاش وتبادل الأفكار واحتكاك بعضها ببعض موافقة أو مخالفة أو برهنة أو معارضة أو تعلما وتعليمًا وما شاكل ذلك مما هو مرتكز في الفطرة الإنسانية وما تستدعيه طبيعة النوع البشري من التعارف والمدنية فالجدال لا يمكن أن يخلو منه بشر عنده بيان لأنه يختلج في نفسه من بيان بصرف النظر عن طبيعة هذا البيان وبواعثه. إن الجدل – إذا – إنما ينشأ حيث يقع الاختلاف في إدراك حقائق الحياة بين بني الإنسان، سواء تعلقت هذه الحقائق بما يعتقده الإنسان عن الكون وخالقه وطبيعة العلاقة التي تربطه بهما، أو تعلقت بكيفية السلوك في الواقع وما ينبغي فعله أو الكف عنه، ذلك أن الجدل إنما يعود بمعناه العام إلى نزعة البيان والإفصاح المودعة فطريًا في الإنسان، وهو عبارة عما يجيش في النفس من تأثرات وأحاسيس لإظهار مبدأ أو نصرة حق أو تصحيح خطأ أو توجيه مفهوم أو غير ذلك مما جبلت عليه النفوس البشرية وبأي دافع كان هذا الحوار الإنساني فالجدل ضرورة حتمية بمعناه العام أي أنه طبيعة وجبلة في الفطرة الإنسانية. وليس هذا المبدأ الفطري حكرًا على الراشدين فقط من بني الإنسان دون صغارهم وصبيانهم، بل إنه في صوره البسيطة الساذجة نجده منتشرا بكثرة بين الصبيان في ألعابهم ومحاوراتهم، إذ عادة ما تنشأ المحاورات الجدلية بينهم إذا سطا أحدهم مثلا على شيء لآخر، فيضطر صاحبه إلى الدفاع عن حقه في ذلك.
The first section: Jadal is an innate tendency in humans. The first sub-section: Jadal is a universal phenomenon: God Almighty created man and instilled in him - among what He instilled - the ability to contemplate and think, which is the result of wonder and questioning that occur in the human mind when observing the creatures around him in this universe of different types, shapes, and functions, as well as his attempt to understand himself and the essence of his existence and the relationship that connects him to the universe and what is in it. And just as God, glorified and exalted, instilled in man questioning and thinking, He also instilled in him the ability to speak about what his contemplation and thinking reach, for he finds himself driven by nature and disposition to love to express his thoughts and information and to disclose them. And if this is a tangible innate reality, there is another innate reality about which no two differ, which is that diversity is a human reality that begins from the difference in organic composition among humans - male and female - and ends with dozens of images that reflect this difference and diversity - in colors, natures, temperaments, lifestyles, behaviors, etc. - and this diversity is the superior characteristic of this human being, and it is the natural result of the elements of freedom and choice that distinguish the human being. And this diversity at all these levels leads - naturally - to diversity in what a person reaches through his contemplation and thinking. Therefore, intellectual difference among humans is as old as man on this earth; it began when he began to look at the universe, amazed by its greatness, and seized by bewilderment in perceiving its essence and reality. Then man, in addition to being naturally disposed to diversity and intellectual difference, is also naturally disposed to interaction with others of his kind through contact and acquaintance, as God Almighty said: 'O mankind, indeed We have created you from male and female and made you peoples and tribes that you may know one another. Indeed, the most noble of you in the sight of God is the most righteous. Indeed, God is Knowing and Acquainted.' And it is natural that this interaction results in the convergence of ideas, their collision, and their conflict, for every person finds himself driven to express his thoughts and ideas, to disclose them, and to present them for dialogue and debate, resulting in argumentation, discussion, exchange of ideas, and their interaction with each other, whether in agreement or disagreement, proof or opposition, learning or teaching, and the like, all of which are rooted in human nature and necessitated by the nature of the human species of acquaintance and civilization. So debate cannot be absent from any human who has expression, because it stirs within him from expression regardless of the nature of this expression and its motives. Thus, jadal arises wherever there is difference in perceiving the realities of life among humans, whether these realities pertain to what man believes about the universe and its Creator and the nature of the relationship connecting him to them, or pertain to how to behave in reality and what should be done or refrained from. That is because jadal in its general sense goes back to the innate tendency of expression and disclosure deposited naturally in man, and it is what surges in the soul of impressions and feelings to manifest a principle, support a truth, correct an error, direct a concept, or other things for which human souls are disposed, and whatever the motive for this human dialogue, jadal is an inevitable necessity in its general sense, meaning it is a nature and disposition in human nature. And this innate principle is not restricted only to adults among humans to the exclusion of children and youths; rather, in its simple naive forms, we find it widespread among children in their games and conversations, as argumentative dialogues usually arise among them when, for example, one of them encroaches on something belonging to another, so the owner is forced to defend his right to it.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a front_matter introduction establishing the natural foundation of jadal (dialectic) in humans. The author argues that jadal is an innate, universal human phenomenon rooted in creation. The argument proceeds by first noting that God created humans with the capacity for contemplation and questioning, which leads to wonder and seeking understanding. Then, humans are also endowed with speech to express their thoughts. Next, the author highlights diversity in human nature—physical, temperamental, etc.—which naturally leads to intellectual diversity. This diversity, combined with the human tendency for interaction and acquaintance (citing Quran 49:13), inevitably brings ideas into contact, conflict, and debate. Thus, jadal emerges wherever there is difference in perceiving life's realities, whether theological or practical. The author emphasizes that jadal in its general sense is the innate drive to express and argue for principles, truth, correction, or guidance. Finally, the author extends this to children, showing that even simple disputes among them demonstrate the naturalness of jadal. The passage establishes jadal as a fundamental human trait necessary for social and intellectual life.

The Qur'an's Call for Jadal
Author discusses the prevalence of jadal in the Qur'an, especially in Meccan period.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Qur'an commands Muslims to engage in jadal with disbelievers and People of the Book.

Core Idea
Jadal in the Qur'an is a commanded method for guiding others to truth.

In this passage, the author highlights that Allah explicitly commands Muslims to argue (jadal) with both the People of the Book and disbelievers. But why would a divine scripture command argumentation? The answer lies in the purpose: to bring them back to the straight path. Jadal is presented as a tool of guidance, not mere debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the divine command
Locate where the author states that God urged Muslims to engage in jadal. Notice the dual audience: People of the Book and disbelievers.
2
Understand the purpose
The goal is 'hoping to bring them back to the straight path.' Jadal is thus a means of da'wa.
3
Recognize the broader claim
The author then expands that jadal is not limited to those verses but is pervasive throughout the Qur'an as a method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حث الله المسلمين في هذه الآيات المتعددة، إخبارًا وأمرًا، على مجادلة أهل الكتاب والكفار على حد سواء، وذلك رجاء إرجاعهم إلى الطريق السوي والصراط المستقيم (1) . والواقع أن مادة الجدل في القرآن الكريم لا تتوقف عند هذا العدد المحدود من الآيات، بل إنها تنساب في سور القرآن الكريم كلها، صحيح أن المادة اللغوية (ج) ، د ، ل لا تذكر إلا في هذه المرات المذكورة، إلا أن الجدل كمادة فكرية أو كمنهج، موجود بكثرة في القرآن الكريم، حتى إننا نكاد نقول إن القرآن هو كتاب جدل عن العقيدة والشريعة . ولربما كانت المرحلة المكية من نزول القرآن أكثر احتفالا بالجدل كأسلوب في خطاب الكفار والمشركين، حيث اختص العهد المكي بسبع عشرة آية تعرضت لذكر الجدل، وذلك لأن طبيعة هذه المرحلة بالذات وما تميزت به من مقاصد أساسية تود الدعوة إثباتها وترسيخها، تستلزم إثارة الموضوع قصدًا أو عرضًا، فعقيدة التوحيد يتطلب تأسيسها إقامة الشواهد المثبتة وادعاء النبوة يفرض التصديق به تقديم المؤيدات المقنعة، والإيمان باليوم الآخر يتوقف قبوله على إبانة الحجج القاطعة، بالإضافة إلى أن هناك عوائق معنوية متمكنة يقتضي اقتلاعها عقد مناظرات مفحمة وعراقيل مادية متحكمة يحتم تضييق الخناق عليها تنظيم محاورات ومناقشات تبكيتية»(2) ولا يعني هذا أن المرحلة المدنية قد خلت من الطابع الجدلي، بل إنها حفلت الأخرى بمجادلة اليهود والنصارى، لكن بشكل أقل مما حدث مع المشركين في المرحلة المكية التي كان التركيز فيها منصبا على غرس العقيدة وإثباتها بمختلف الوسائل الإقناعية .. وقد استقرأ الإمام نجم الدين الطوفي نماذج الجدل في القرآن الكريم، فعرض علينا عشرات الصور الجدلية من أكثر من خمسين سورة من سور القرآن الكريم مكية ومدنية على سواء
God urged Muslims in these multiple verses, both as information and command, to argue with the People of the Book and the disbelievers equally, hoping to bring them back to the straight path and the right way (1). Indeed, the topic of jadal in the Noble Qur'an does not stop at this limited number of verses, but rather it flows throughout all the surahs of the Noble Qur'an. It is true that the linguistic root (j, d, l) is only mentioned in these aforementioned instances, but jadal as an intellectual subject or as a method is present abundantly in the Noble Qur'an, so much so that we almost say that the Qur'an is a book of jadal about creed and law. Perhaps the Meccan stage of the revelation of the Qur'an was more celebratory of jadal as a method in addressing the disbelievers and polytheists, as the Meccan period was characterized by seventeen verses that dealt with the mention of jadal, because the nature of this particular stage and its fundamental objectives that the call wanted to prove and establish required raising the subject intentionally or incidentally. The creed of tawhid requires establishing proofs to confirm it, the claim of prophethood requires presenting convincing evidences for its acceptance, and belief in the Last Day depends on presenting decisive arguments. Additionally, there were deep-rooted moral obstacles whose removal required holding refuting debates and dominating material obstacles that necessitated tightening the noose on them by organizing persuasive discussions and debates (2). This does not mean that the Medinan stage was devoid of the jadal character; rather, it also abounded with arguing with the Jews and Christians, but to a lesser extent than what happened with the polytheists in the Meccan stage, which focused on implanting the creed and proving it by various persuasive means. Imam Najm al-Din al-Tufi reviewed examples of jadal in the Noble Qur'an and presented us with dozens of jadal images from more than fifty surahs of the Noble Qur'an, both Meccan and Medinan alike.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of jadal in the Qur'an. The author begins by stating that God commanded Muslims to argue with both the People of the Book and disbelievers, aiming to guide them. He then emphasizes that the concept of jadal is not limited to the few verses where the root (j-d-l) appears; rather, the entire Qur'an is imbued with the method of jadal as an intellectual approach to defend creed and law. The author notes that the Meccan period, focused on establishing the fundamentals of faith (tawhid, prophethood, afterlife), made heavy use of jadal against polytheists. The Medinan period also used jadal, particularly against Jews and Christians, but less intensively. The passage concludes by citing Imam al-Tufi's extensive survey of jadal examples across the Qur'an. The commentary should highlight that jadal is presented as a divine method for proving truth, not mere argumentation.

المرحلة المكية
المرحلة المدنية

Meccan vs Medinan Jadal: Emphasis and Context
Author discusses the prevalence of jadal in the Qur'an, especially in Meccan period.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the different emphases of jadal in the Meccan and Medinan periods and explain why.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's jadal adapts to the audience and objectives: Meccan surahs focus on creed, Medinan on community.

The passage explains that while jadal is present throughout the Qur'an, its intensity differs between the Meccan and Medinan periods. The Meccan period, concerned with establishing the foundations of faith, employed jadal extensively against polytheists. In contrast, the Medinan period, while still using jadal against Jews and Christians, did so less intensively. Understanding this context helps interpret the style of different surahs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Meccan period's focus
The author lists three foundational beliefs (tawhid, prophethood, afterlife) that required proof. Notice that these are exactly the issues polytheists denied.
2
Understand the challenges
The Meccan period also faced moral and material obstacles that required forceful debates.
3
Contrast with Medinan
The Medinan period involved jadal but mainly with Jews and Christians, and less intensely because the community was already established.
4
Check the evidence
The author notes that seventeen Meccan verses mention the root (j-d-l), and al-Tufi found examples in over fifty surahs overall, showing the method's breadth.

حث الله المسلمين في هذه الآيات المتعددة، إخبارًا وأمرًا، على مجادلة أهل الكتاب والكفار على حد سواء، وذلك رجاء إرجاعهم إلى الطريق السوي والصراط المستقيم (1) . والواقع أن مادة الجدل في القرآن الكريم لا تتوقف عند هذا العدد المحدود من الآيات، بل إنها تنساب في سور القرآن الكريم كلها، صحيح أن المادة اللغوية (ج) ، د ، ل لا تذكر إلا في هذه المرات المذكورة، إلا أن الجدل كمادة فكرية أو كمنهج، موجود بكثرة في القرآن الكريم، حتى إننا نكاد نقول إن القرآن هو كتاب جدل عن العقيدة والشريعة . ولربما كانت المرحلة المكية من نزول القرآن أكثر احتفالا بالجدل كأسلوب في خطاب الكفار والمشركين، حيث اختص العهد المكي بسبع عشرة آية تعرضت لذكر الجدل، وذلك لأن طبيعة هذه المرحلة بالذات وما تميزت به من مقاصد أساسية تود الدعوة إثباتها وترسيخها، تستلزم إثارة الموضوع قصدًا أو عرضًا، فعقيدة التوحيد يتطلب تأسيسها إقامة الشواهد المثبتة وادعاء النبوة يفرض التصديق به تقديم المؤيدات المقنعة، والإيمان باليوم الآخر يتوقف قبوله على إبانة الحجج القاطعة، بالإضافة إلى أن هناك عوائق معنوية متمكنة يقتضي اقتلاعها عقد مناظرات مفحمة وعراقيل مادية متحكمة يحتم تضييق الخناق عليها تنظيم محاورات ومناقشات تبكيتية»(2) ولا يعني هذا أن المرحلة المدنية قد خلت من الطابع الجدلي، بل إنها حفلت الأخرى بمجادلة اليهود والنصارى، لكن بشكل أقل مما حدث مع المشركين في المرحلة المكية التي كان التركيز فيها منصبا على غرس العقيدة وإثباتها بمختلف الوسائل الإقناعية .. وقد استقرأ الإمام نجم الدين الطوفي نماذج الجدل في القرآن الكريم، فعرض علينا عشرات الصور الجدلية من أكثر من خمسين سورة من سور القرآن الكريم مكية ومدنية على سواء
God urged Muslims in these multiple verses, both as information and command, to argue with the People of the Book and the disbelievers equally, hoping to bring them back to the straight path and the right way (1). Indeed, the topic of jadal in the Noble Qur'an does not stop at this limited number of verses, but rather it flows throughout all the surahs of the Noble Qur'an. It is true that the linguistic root (j, d, l) is only mentioned in these aforementioned instances, but jadal as an intellectual subject or as a method is present abundantly in the Noble Qur'an, so much so that we almost say that the Qur'an is a book of jadal about creed and law. Perhaps the Meccan stage of the revelation of the Qur'an was more celebratory of jadal as a method in addressing the disbelievers and polytheists, as the Meccan period was characterized by seventeen verses that dealt with the mention of jadal, because the nature of this particular stage and its fundamental objectives that the call wanted to prove and establish required raising the subject intentionally or incidentally. The creed of tawhid requires establishing proofs to confirm it, the claim of prophethood requires presenting convincing evidences for its acceptance, and belief in the Last Day depends on presenting decisive arguments. Additionally, there were deep-rooted moral obstacles whose removal required holding refuting debates and dominating material obstacles that necessitated tightening the noose on them by organizing persuasive discussions and debates (2). This does not mean that the Medinan stage was devoid of the jadal character; rather, it also abounded with arguing with the Jews and Christians, but to a lesser extent than what happened with the polytheists in the Meccan stage, which focused on implanting the creed and proving it by various persuasive means. Imam Najm al-Din al-Tufi reviewed examples of jadal in the Noble Qur'an and presented us with dozens of jadal images from more than fifty surahs of the Noble Qur'an, both Meccan and Medinan alike.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of jadal in the Qur'an. The author begins by stating that God commanded Muslims to argue with both the People of the Book and disbelievers, aiming to guide them. He then emphasizes that the concept of jadal is not limited to the few verses where the root (j-d-l) appears; rather, the entire Qur'an is imbued with the method of jadal as an intellectual approach to defend creed and law. The author notes that the Meccan period, focused on establishing the fundamentals of faith (tawhid, prophethood, afterlife), made heavy use of jadal against polytheists. The Medinan period also used jadal, particularly against Jews and Christians, but less intensively. The passage concludes by citing Imam al-Tufi's extensive survey of jadal examples across the Qur'an. The commentary should highlight that jadal is presented as a divine method for proving truth, not mere argumentation.

المرحلة المكية
المرحلة المدنية

Introduction to Jadal in the Sunnah
Author introduces the topic of jadal in the Sunnah and previews three examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relationship between Quranic jadal and prophetic jadal, and recognize the characteristics of jadal in the Sunnah as described by the author.

Core Idea
The Sunnah is the practical embodiment of Quranic jadal, where the Prophet clarified truth with brevity.

This passage introduces the second major source of jadal: the purified Sunnah. The author explains that the Prophet's method of dialectical argumentation is concise and clear, often involving dialogues with his Companions. This lesson sets the stage for the specific examples that follow, highlighting the legitimacy and importance of jadal in Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The author explicitly states that jadal is 'revealed upon' the Sunnah, indicating a direct influence from Quranic jadal. Note the phrase 'al-jadal al-munazzal 'alayhi'.
This establishes the Sunnah as a divinely guided source of argumentation.
2
Identify the author's promise
The author says he will present examples (namādhij) from the Sunnah that demonstrate how the Prophet clarified truth. Look for the words 'sanuridu fīmā yalī'.
The examples are the core of the subsequent lesson.
3
Note the characteristic of prophetic jadal
The Prophet clarified truth with 'awjaz ‘ibārah wa akhṣar ṭarīq' - the briefest expression and shortest path. This is a key feature of jadal in the Sunnah.
Brevity and clarity are hallmarks of Prophetic argumentation.
4
Recognize the significance
Most examples involve the Prophet and his Companions, which increases the importance and legitimacy of the dialectical method. The author emphasizes this to show that jadal was practiced by the best generation.
This reinforces that jadal is a praiseworthy tool, not mere disputation.

Political Jadal Among the Sahaba
Author describes political disputes among Sahaba over caliphate succession and governance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two phases of political jadal among the Sahaba and distinguish them from later legal jadal.

Core Idea
Historical jadal among the Sahaba had both political and legal dimensions.

This passage outlines the historical context of jadal (disputation) among the Sahaba. The author notes two phases: first, disputes over who deserves the caliphate, and second, disputes over governance and conditions for deposing a caliph. Although the author considers this political jadal outside his research focus, understanding it is crucial for appreciating the broader development of Islamic debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first type of jadal
Find where the author mentions jadal about 'who is more entitled to the caliphate'. This is the initial phase of political dispute.
2
Identify the second type of jadal
Locate the second phase: jadal about 'how to conduct the affairs of the caliphate' and 'conditions for deposal'.
3
Notice the author's distinction
The author explicitly says this political jadal is 'not important for us to delve into' because it does not relate to his research. Recognize that he is setting it aside.
This signals that his focus is on legal jadal, not political history.
4
Relate to later formal jadal
Understand that later Islamic disciplines (usul al-fiqh, kalam) developed formal rules for jadal. The political debates among Sahaba provided raw material, but they were not methodologically systematized.
The shift from historical disputation to formal dialectical method is key.
